<div id="review_1495068269" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1495068269" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1495068269?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/677922.The_Power_and_the_Glory">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=great-britain">great-britain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1495068269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5099899809893517550">After being received into the Roman Catholic Church Graham Greene would some years later travel to Mexico in 1938 to report and witness first hand the persecution of the clergy, this would clearly go on to have a major impact in writing 'The Power and the Glory', which sees an unnamed Priest (known to locals as the 'whisky Priest') go on the run from the authorities during a time of religious hostilities where many Priests were tried for treason and shot, with only his mule and little in the way</span>
  <span id="freeText5099899809893517550" style="display:none">After being received into the Roman Catholic Church Graham Greene would some years later travel to Mexico in 1938 to report and witness first hand the persecution of the clergy, this would clearly go on to have a major impact in writing 'The Power and the Glory', which sees an unnamed Priest (known to locals as the 'whisky Priest') go on the run from the authorities during a time of religious hostilities where many Priests were tried for treason and shot, with only his mule and little in the way of supplies he navigates the harsh terrain trying to evade capture, and it's the humane and compassionate poor folk of small towns and villages along the way that help to keep him safe even while being offered a reward for his arrest, all the while the Priest is struggling with his own demons and bringing into question his faith and that of those around him. At times things get pretty tense where capture seems inevitable only for him to somehow escape, but I never felt he was in anyway afraid of his outcome and that his life was basically in the hands of God to decide his fate. Although written as a work of fiction I would not be surprised if some content was based on fact, and the writing is highly believable and impeccable throughout, as my first Graham Greene novel I can fully understand why he was considered one of the greats.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5099899809893517550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2639419885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2639419885" />
    <a title="Monika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38021482-monika"><img alt="Monika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581038684p2/38021482.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2639419885?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monika" href="/user/show/38021482-monika">Monika</a>
      </span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2639419885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12477837133352295927"><i>The Power and the Glory</i> is a powerful and a glorious story. Set as a consequence of Cristero War, the novel revolves majorly around the journey of a whisky priest, a term coined by Graham Greene. An attempt by Mexican government to suppress the Catholic Church was in full swing. As a result, the lieutenant comes up with a plan so that he can follow the government's order.<br/><br/>This novel is essentially about perspectives and kindness in the face of the barren world. It is about mutual respect to diffe</span>
  <span id="freeText12477837133352295927" style="display:none"><i>The Power and the Glory</i> is a powerful and a glorious story. Set as a consequence of Cristero War, the novel revolves majorly around the journey of a whisky priest, a term coined by Graham Greene. An attempt by Mexican government to suppress the Catholic Church was in full swing. As a result, the lieutenant comes up with a plan so that he can follow the government's order.<br /><br />This novel is essentially about perspectives and kindness in the face of the barren world. It is about mutual respect to differing ideas. It is about regretting for some deeds, while not regretting for the result of those deeds. It is about abandonment and hushed secrets. It is about recurring ghostly presences. All in all, it is a wasteland that the modern world has continued to be.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12477837133352295927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2550185680" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2550185680" />
    <a title="Zoeb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78712152-zoeb"><img alt="Zoeb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520855238p2/78712152.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2550185680?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zoeb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zoeb" href="/user/show/78712152-zoeb">Zoeb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2550185680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer597713845742494099">I wish I could write like Graham Greene. <br/><br/>Actually, I take that back. I wish I could see the world and chronicle it as Greene did. And I wish, oh how I wish, that I could believe like Greene. <br/><br/>'The Power And The Glory' is lauded widely (and deservedly so) as Greene's masterpiece and while some hard-nosed critics and snobs call it a 'Catholic novel' merely because its primary protagonist is a priest and it deals primarily with the said priest's struggle to keep the flag of his faith flying even in </span>
  <span id="freeText597713845742494099" style="display:none">I wish I could write like Graham Greene. <br /><br />Actually, I take that back. I wish I could see the world and chronicle it as Greene did. And I wish, oh how I wish, that I could believe like Greene. <br /><br />'The Power And The Glory' is lauded widely (and deservedly so) as Greene's masterpiece and while some hard-nosed critics and snobs call it a 'Catholic novel' merely because its primary protagonist is a priest and it deals primarily with the said priest's struggle to keep the flag of his faith flying even in the oppressive atmosphere of Mexico in the days of the Red Shirts, it is important to note that the novel transcends effortlessly the very limited scope of a real Catholic novel. I stepped into Greene's book with exhilaration but also a faint sense of apprehension; an overtly Catholic novel, while undoubtedly intriguing, could also be a bit inaccessible, unfathomable even in all its moral conundrums and intriguing spiritual arguments. However, right from the first page, reeking of the stark desolation and disillusionment that marks many a page in this 200+page magnum opus, we know that we are in the hands of Greene again, more compellingly than ever, the tale writ large over his customary canvas of a morally grey landscape that almost feels like dystopia.<br /><br />Our protagonist is a priest. Greene does not give him a name or even much of a description apart from the stray discerning glances that he lends every now and then. Faces and identities, like so much other seemingly pivotal detail, are of no great significance; like a Biblical narrative, 'The Power And The Glory' reads like a brooding yet frequently eye-gouging nihilistic spectacle played out over a ruthless, sun-baked and sweltering land where this priest is forced to take flight and keep on plodding ahead for his life. Christianity, or rather religion and belief of God itself, is deemed as taboo and treason in this alienating and desolate part of a savagely beautiful country and priests are either shamed into mortal sin and lazy complacency or shot down like political prisoners. Yet, the priest goes on, doing his work, sometimes out of reluctance, sometimes out of a misplaced, naively genereous passion for the downtrodden and desperate who seek his succour. <br /><br />Alongside Greene, however, it is God who is also at the helm of this strange yet stirringly grand narrative. It is well said that 'Man proposes and God disposes' and whenever Greene grants his flawed yet obstinately proud priest a chance, a fair shot at redemption or even escape, it is God who seems to intervene, deciding otherwise. 'The Power And The Glory', more than any other Greene novel (and so many, from 'The Heart Of The Matter' to 'The Human Factor' to even 'The Honorary Consul' come brilliantly close to it), explains with potent force and profundity the predicament and despair of mortal man at the mercy of metaphysical forces and the strange workings of the soul itself, of the irreparable devastation that faith, or the obsessive, even toxic belief in an ideal both noble and ruthless, can bring on the soul. <br /><br />And yet, what makes 'The Power And The Glory' frequently uplifting and invigorating, even as every moment of possible release and redemption darkens spectacularly into doom, is Greene's strident insistence that we need to believe, if not in Christianity then in some faith that concedes to the acceptance of God's eye-widening miracles. Unfairly attacked by the Church for being heretical, here is instead a rousing vindication of the very essential belief in an existence and omnipotence of God and yet Greene is no mere preacher from a pulpit. He is instead a warrior poet with a soul of subtlety and compassion and even as the novel culminates in heartbreak and catharsis, there is always a little but nevertheless all too distinct room for hope. Some have likened it to a modern Cruxificion parable and the metaphors and similarities are unmistakable and the even the darkest scenes in the narrative bring such a surging force of emotion that it is hard not to be swept along. <br /><br />As I started reading 'The Power And The Glory', I had been talking to a friend who had read it a long time ago and he remembered that the first time he finished the book, he thought of it to be dystopia. Indeed, as ever with the powerfully and prophetically prescient Greene, this is set in a specific country in a specific epoch of chaos and darkness but it is more than just about Mexico in the 1930s. It is rather indeed as powerful a dystopian portrait of mankind brought to its knees by a regime that uproots the very nourishment of the human soul as any can be. Greene's portrait of that country is one rendered in telling and deeply nuanced strokes; any other writer would have been content to indulge in exotic scenery but the characters that Greene populates his world with, as an evidence of his peerless storytelling abilities and astute command of craft, are not merely literary stereotypes; they might not have faces, identities or even backstories but they have flesh, blood and souls that makes them so compelling. And as always, Greene's prose is flawless, beautiful and dramatic without exaggeration or excess. Not a word is out of place, not a trope is repeated; like Hemingway, the words sing lithely yet profoundly. Just that in Greene's case, the words are even more beautiful. <br /><br />Is 'The Power And The Glory' Greene's finest novel yet? The fact that we are pondering over this question is itself the answer. Read it for yourself to believe just how powerful and glorious it is. <br /><br />Hallelujah!</span>
  <a data-text-id="597713845742494099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1238336835" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1238336835" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1238336835?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1238336835" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4253728317990281245">A truly great book and (forgive the use of what is probably a very well –worn cliché) a novel that is without a doubt powerful and glorious on many levels. <br/><br/>Set in Mexico in the 1930’s against the backdrop of an attempted suppression of the Catholic Church by the authorities. Ostensibly this is the story of the fugitive, renegade ‘Whiskey Priest’ (a great creation and a believably authentic character) and his quest to escape the anti-Catholic authorities. This is a novel that confronts head-on th</span>
  <span id="freeText4253728317990281245" style="display:none">A truly great book and (forgive the use of what is probably a very well –worn cliché) a novel that is without a doubt powerful and glorious on many levels. <br /><br />Set in Mexico in the 1930’s against the backdrop of an attempted suppression of the Catholic Church by the authorities. Ostensibly this is the story of the fugitive, renegade ‘Whiskey Priest’ (a great creation and a believably authentic character) and his quest to escape the anti-Catholic authorities. This is a novel that confronts head-on the biggest of themes: sin, redemption, salvation, damnation, heaven, hell and practically everything else in between. Also encompassed here is the dogmatic approach of both organised religion and the authorities attempting to not just to crush and outlaw, but to obliterate that religion – the pitfalls, limitations, restrictions and constraints of any rigidly authoritarian belief system. But the story here is not merely as simple and straightforward as one of ‘religion vs anti-religion’ – far from it.<br /><br />Essentially it seems to me that the ’Whiskey Priest’ (our hero or anti-hero? – you choose) isn’t just a representation or metaphor for the Catholic Church; to me the power of this novel and the characters within goes way beyond the constraints of religion – surely the ‘Whiskey Priest’ is everyman and the story every life – comprised as they are of hopes, fears, desires, dreams, failures, victories, disappointments, faith and doubt… <br /><br />Graham Greene himself visited Mexico in the 1930’s and would have witnessed and been aware of the attempted suppression of the Catholic Church – something which will undoubtedly have informed elements of this novel. Green didn’t however view himself as a ‘Catholic author’ rather more – an author who happened to be Catholic. Religion as a theme, in many different ways, does seem more than evident in many of his novels. Certainly religion in this novel is considered in many ways – religion and its suppressors, organised religion juxtaposed with religion on a far more personal basis. <br /><br />‘The Power and The Glory’ is simply a great and powerful work – which I look forward to reading again, as there is so much in it to fully take in and take away. It is a novel that is so well written, constructed, plotted and thought out - moving, authentic, intelligent and thought provoking. <br /><br />This is a very human story, it’s about the human condition and above all else – it’s about life and death, good and evil, hate and love and above all – hope. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4253728317990281245" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_77693454" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780140017915" href="/book/show/1703614.The_Power_and_the_Glory">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      501 and 1001 Must Read Books

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer77693454&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer77693454" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3395660730226045264">I read this book during my 3-day visit in San Diego and it was an appropriate choice because of the proximity of the place to Mexico and there are more Mexicans in that place than causcasians. This book is considered by many novelists as Graham Greene's masterpiece and I think they are right.<br/><br/>This is a story of a nameless Catholic priest who is pious but at the same time alcoholic and fathered a child. These may not be shocking at the present time but this novel created a scandal in the catholic </span>
  <span id="freeText3395660730226045264" style="display:none">I read this book during my 3-day visit in San Diego and it was an appropriate choice because of the proximity of the place to Mexico and there are more Mexicans in that place than causcasians. This book is considered by many novelists as Graham Greene's masterpiece and I think they are right.<br /><br />This is a story of a nameless Catholic priest who is pious but at the same time alcoholic and fathered a child. These may not be shocking at the present time but this novel created a scandal in the catholic world when it was asked by a cardinal of Westminster to be revised including the two other novels of the same author. The setting of the story was in Mexico when the government, in the 30's was trying to eradicate Catholicism in the country. <br /><br />The main two characters are that priest (the last one standing) and the lieutenant who was able to arrest and prosecute the priest towards the end of the story. However, prior to the final scene another priest came up that gave the hint that the catholicism was there to survive in Mexico.<br /><br />What I really liked about the story is the presentation of the characters. The 'human' character of the priest was not hidden for the sake of making him saint-like. Also, the character of the lieutenant was also not all evil. In fact, in most parts of the story he made more sense that the priest except when he was killing people for the priest to surface.<br /><br />This book is both in the 501 and 1001 Must Read Books and indeed it is right to be there.</span>

<div id="review_2849900859" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2849900859" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2849900859?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/30840015-the-power-and-the-glory">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=uk-irish">uk-irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2849900859" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12448339095164209500">"The blood of the martyrs is the seed of the Church".<br/>Thus Tertullian wrote in Apologeticus, chapter 50. He kind of knew what he was talking about, as he lived long before the glorious days of triumphant Christianity - long before Constantine's mother started throwing tantrums and her son realised Pope Sylvester was not merely fucking around with chalices and wafers ("In hoc signo vinces": how about that, Marshall McLuhan?). <br/>Like it or not, Tertullian's words summarise the essence of Christianity</span>
  <span id="freeText12448339095164209500" style="display:none">"The blood of the martyrs is the seed of the Church".<br />Thus Tertullian wrote in Apologeticus, chapter 50. He kind of knew what he was talking about, as he lived long before the glorious days of triumphant Christianity - long before Constantine's mother started throwing tantrums and her son realised Pope Sylvester was not merely fucking around with chalices and wafers ("In hoc signo vinces": how about that, Marshall McLuhan?). <br />Like it or not, Tertullian's words summarise the essence of Christianity better than the whole Summa Theologiæ. <br /><br />No doubt Christianity was born of a bloodshed; it also thrived in it, though. I daresay it owes everything to it. Its most sacred rite is an act of sublimated cannibalism we inherited from the human butchery of ancient Canaan. The Christian God offered His Only Son (capital letters are compulsory) so that we could stop slaughtering our own firstborn children and wash our sins in their blood. <br />Let's face it: although not overtly nuts as the worshipers of Kali, we've always been quite obsessed with blood. The early Christians went through persecution with a feverish lust for physical and psychological abuse. It was not death as such that could satisfy their hunger for holiness: it had to be long, painful and humiliating. To the delight of Krafft-Ebing and Bataille, they had to cross the threshold of Heaven leaving a trail of blood behind them. <br />A couple of centuries later the blood was no longer their own. It was that of their enemies. <br /><br />The problem is, once in a while it happens again. Let aside the internal affairs (schisms, reformations, counter-reformations and settling of accounts) Christianity does have enemies: either it is in Queen Ranavalona's Madagascar or in pre-Commodore Perry Japan, beyond the iron curtain or in the African wilderness, martyrdom is always at hand. If nowadays there's no Nero using the devotees as human torches to illuminate the streets of Rome, it's just because we have subtler means to perform pogroms and murders - namely politics and legal systems. <br />One of the relatively recent persecutions took place in Mexico during Calles' presidency. In 1926 the government declared the Catholic Church to be the source of all evil and started a violent purge against both the clergy and the religious population; all throughout the following decade the people's reaction was fierce and 70.000-85.000 people died in the name of the Father and of the Son and of the Holy Spirit. <br /><br />British journalist and newly converted Graham Green visited Mexico in 1938 to report on the religious persecution and was therefore first-hand witness of the atmosphere of those years. "The Power and the Glory" is the way Greene, looking back at the tragedy one year later, came to terms with his own inner concerns and religious contradictions.<br />The protagonist is a nameless alkie priest wandering through the Mexican waste lands, chased by the government's hangmen and consumed by feelings of inadequacy and sense of guilt. His past is not exactly immaculate indeed, as it seems to validate all the anticlerical stereotypes people is usually fed by hostile ideologues. He considers himself unworthy of everything he's got, even persecution. Suffering in the name of the Lord is an honour he doesn't really deserve, or so he thinks. <br />The thing is, the man's the only priest available. Hungry, exhausted, terrified, reeking of cheap booze, he's the last of his kind and feels the terrible burden of his function - a stigma he just can't rid himself of, despite the overwhelming fear. Isn't that the true nature of faith anyway? In his own words, <br />"To believe in peace was a kind of heresy". <br />In fact hatred and betrayal are everywhere and the enemy is closing in, but he can't surrender, even though the only way out is death: surrender would be a fate worse than death, the ultimate failure in his miserable existence. He's given up on himself already... he's not going to give up on God too. Thus he keeps celebrating the outlawed Christian rites among the poor and the desperate, all along the road leading to his doom. Whereas the respectable clergy chose either escape or betrayal, Greene's wretched priest is called to become a martyr. <br /><br />Heat. Bugs. Toothless mouths. Greasy hair and unwashed bodies. Stinking ratholes and rotting mud huts. By his own admission, Graham Greene wrote most of this book while on benzedrine - and it definitely shows. In a good way, that is.<br />Greene's writing style is elegant and atmospheric; his descriptions of the Mexican landscape are incredibly vivid and real (especially if you read the novel while the temperature is 40°C, drenched in sweat, mosquitos feasting on your legs), not to mention the way he portrays the characters' physical and moral ugliness, which can be compared to the Western Expressionism of Alejandro Jodorowsky's "El Topo" or the (few) gems of what they called Spaghetti Western - that peculiar blend of Italian histrionics and cardboard Americana of the 60s and early 70s. <br />The narration is balanced, with no redundant details nor convoluted subplots. The dialogues are also well-written; all the characters sound natural and the author makes clever use of both flashback and stream of consciousness, with an excellent sense of depth and psychological introspection. <br /><br />However, it would be pointless to draw parallels between Greene's conversion to Catholicism and the subject of his novel. Let alone his motives (he had to so in order to marry a Roman Catholic), the author's attitude toward religion was quite different - he was hardly tormented by the sort of doubts, shame and self-contempt at the core of his priest's personality.<br />Greene was neither a frustrated ascetic nor a repentant sinner; his devotion was more cerebral than emotional. His main interest was in the human dimension of faith, seen as the source of empathy and compassion and therefore as a common experience taking place beyond the confined space of individuality. <br /><br />My first Graham Greene was definitely worth reading. Only thing, I would have liked more blood.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12448339095164209500" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_426122998" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/426122998" />
    <a title="Michael Perkins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins"><img alt="Michael Perkins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454487234p2/13298726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/426122998?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Perkins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Perkins" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins">Michael Perkins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39214757-the-power-and-the-glory">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer426122998" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer424482324529271760">This is a re-read and, boy, no one writes this well any more.<br/><br/>The prison chapter, #3 in Part Two, is an utterly amazing piece of writing. You feel you're right there in the dark, crowded cell with the whiskey priest and the rest of the inmates. This book should be required reading in any college English lit class. <br/><br/>============<br/><br/>Truly haunting.....<br/><br/>"They had travelled by the sun until the black wooded bar of mountain told them where to go. They might have been the only survivors of a world which was </span>
  <span id="freeText424482324529271760" style="display:none">This is a re-read and, boy, no one writes this well any more.<br /><br />The prison chapter, #3 in Part Two, is an utterly amazing piece of writing. You feel you're right there in the dark, crowded cell with the whiskey priest and the rest of the inmates. This book should be required reading in any college English lit class. <br /><br />============<br /><br />Truly haunting.....<br /><br />"They had travelled by the sun until the black wooded bar of mountain told them where to go. They might have been the only survivors of a world which was dying out; they carried the visible marks of the dying with them....At sunset on the second day they came out on to a wide plateau covered with short grass. A grove of crosses stood up blackly against the sky, leaning at different angles— some as high as twenty feet, some not much more than eight. They were like trees that had been left to seed....The evening star was out: it hung low down over the edge of the plateau— it looked as if it was within reach— and a small hot wind stirred." <br /><br />======== <br /><br />The first time I read this novel, many years ago, there was no open pedophile crisis in the Church. My sense is that Greene is portraying the "whiskey priest" in a way that is consistent with Church doctrine, established in the early fifth century as a result of the so-called Donatist controversy. <br /><br />The Donatists were rigorists who argued that Christian clergy must be faultless for their ministry to be effective and their prayers and sacraments to be valid. Augustine of Hippo led the counter effort and the official Church doctrine became: “From the moment that a sacrament is celebrated in accordance with the intention of the Church, the power of Christ and His Spirit acts in and through it, independently of the personal holiness of the minister”<br /><br />This proved quite convenient in modern times when Bishops were able to move around pedophile priests to different parishes to perform the Eucharist, even if these priests were scoundrels.<br /><br />In the novel, we see the consistent contempt of lay people, including children, for the whiskey priest, as a drunk and for having fathered a child out of wedlock, who despises him. I find myself siding with the laity in their contempt for the priest. I can't feel sorry for him. And he learns not to feel sorry for himself.<br /><br />This scorn has all come back again in a different context as much of laity now feels contempt for the whole Church because of its malfeasance, while younger people are drifting away.<br /><br />The Power and the Glory was published in 1940. For all the struggle in the story, it has an idealistic ending. That would not be the case with Greene's "A Burnt-Out Case," published 20 years later. (See link to my review below)<br /><br />=======================<br /><br />the danger of spiritual pride......<br /><br />"It was appalling how easily one forgot and went back; he could still hear his own voice speaking in the street with the Concepción accent— unchanged by mortal sin and unrepentance and desertion. The brandy was musty on the tongue with his own corruption. God might forgive cowardice and passion, but was it possible to forgive the habit of piety?....men like the half-caste could be saved, salvation could strike like lightning at the evil heart, but the habit of piety excluded everything but the evening prayer and the Guild meeting and the feel of humble lips on your gloved hand."<br /><br />But also common human weakness...<br /><br />"He told himself. In time it will be all right, I shall pull up, I only ordered three bottles this time. They will be the last I’ll ever drink, I won’t need drink there— he knew he lied."<br /><br />=======================<br /><br />A concise appreciation of "The Power and the Glory" by John Updike. I would differ with Updike in his list of Greene's best books for not including "The Quiet American." In recent months, I have watched Ken Burns' lengthy documentary on the Vietnam War, twice. That, and the excellent book, "Embers of War," by Fredrik Logevall, reinforce Greene's prescience and accuracy in his novel. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.nybooks.com/articles/1990/08/16/the-passion-of-graham-greene/">https://www.nybooks.com/articles/1990...</a><br /><br />=========================<br /><br />"A Burnt-Out Case" review....<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2078522855">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />======================<br /><br />Graham Greene is part of a tally of deserving writers who did not win the Nobel Prize for Literature. The list also includes Tolstoy, Marcel Proust, James Joyce, Virginia Woolf, Jorge Luis Borges, Nabokov, W.H. Auden, Primo Levi, Chinua Achebe, and John Updike.<br /><br />Greene and Borges made the short list in 1967. But to show how subjective the prize could be, a reporter from the London Times interviewed one of the longest-standing and likely most influential members of the Nobel Committee, Artur Lundkvist. When pressed by the reporter re: Greene, Lundkvist actually said: "Greene is too popular, and anyway he doesn't need the money."<br /><br />It's interesting to look at the list and see how many of these writers you may never have heard of.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Literature">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of...</a><br /><br />========<br /><br />A favorite Greene quote....<br /><br />“Doubt is the heart of the matter. Abolish all doubt, and what's left is not faith, but absolute, heartless conviction. You're certain that you possess the Truth -- inevitably offered with an implied uppercase T -- and this certainty quickly devolves into dogmatism and righteousness, by which I mean a demonstrative, overweening pride in being so very right, in short, the arrogance of fundamentalism.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="424482324529271760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_60460310" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60460310" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1719442-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451874923p2/1719442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60460310?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/1719442-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jen by: </span>
      Montambo
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=spirituality-religion">spirituality-religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=lit-fic">lit-fic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer60460310&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60460310" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11072174412833908748"> I like books that have more questions than answers. But the questions have to be good, like this:<br/> <i> "... she unhooked the child and held the face against the wood...Did she expect a miracle? And if she did, why should it not be granted her?...The priest found himself watching the child for some movement. When none came, it was as if God had missed an opportunity. Why, after all, should we expect God to punish the innocent with more life?"</i><br/><br/> The Power and the Glory started off slow and stifling. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText11072174412833908748" style="display:none"> I like books that have more questions than answers. But the questions have to be good, like this:<br /> <i> "... she unhooked the child and held the face against the wood...Did she expect a miracle? And if she did, why should it not be granted her?...The priest found himself watching the child for some movement. When none came, it was as if God had missed an opportunity. Why, after all, should we expect God to punish the innocent with more life?"</i><br /><br /> The Power and the Glory started off slow and stifling. The air is hot, and the sound of a fly hovering around your ear is about all you get. There is no water. A scraggly guy shows up and talks to a mediocre dentist. Ho hum. Then the scraggly guy is off, somehow needed at a dying person's side. Somewhere in the jungle lightning cracks. The air shifts. The world of the book cracks open and the ground rumbles. And suddenly, tiny razor cuts of prose start to sting. You're alive, the book's alive, the scraggly man is alive. The pain is an exquisite reminder of reality and humanity. The scraggly man is Christ in the stranger's garb. Hunted, maligned, but pressing onward, riding a donkey, shunned by his own family and surrendered to forces beyond himself that he only wishes he could fight fairer and without drops of whiskey.<br /><br /> Obviously, I see this man being shown as a fool for the divine- a holy fool-the biblical kind of foolishness that somehow, like many Old Testament prophets, hits square on the center of truth and blackens the eye with true humility despite, because of, or combined with crazy assed behavior like being covered with matted animal skins, mouths dirty with smeared honey and locust tidbits. Am I reading too much into Greene? Consider this, the sacrificial servant passage:<br /><br /><i> "If he left them, they would be safe:and they would be free from his example:he was the only priest the children could remember. It was from him they would take their ideas of the faith. But it was from him too they took God - in their mouths. When he was gone it would be as if God in all this space between the sea and mountains ceased to exist. Wasn't it his duty to stay, even if they despised him, even if they were murdered for his sake, even if they were corrupted by his example? He was shaken by the enormity of the problem: he lay with his hands over his eyes: nowhere, in all the wide flat marshy land, was there a single person he could consult...." </i><br /><br /> But this is not the only thing to see through Greene's looking glass. There's more- that right and wrong/good and evil is not a black and white road as the Catholic Church seemed to at times imply, but a gray path through a hard wilderness- that evil people can do very good things and good people can do very bad things. The sinner and the saint come in unlikely bodies and their spirits cross. And this is reality and it is also the most fantastical fantasy. <br /><br /> Authors who deal with religious themes often include characters that, to use the lyrics of a CAKE song, "shine like justice." Greene's whiskey priest character doesn't do this. He wrestles with the divine, like Jacob with the angel the whole night through and when he awakes he finds that, like Jacob, he is limping. The priest doesn't float a foot off the muddy ground blessedly sermonizing to all he meets full of the awareness of his own power among the people, sanctified with a golden ring over his head. He walks through the mud, for years hides out among them, plodding along through life shitting and eating and fucking just like everyone else, making mistakes and trying to find meaning in the mess of it all. Greene takes characters, gives them flesh, feeds them on the tree of the knowledge of good and evil and then watches them fall like the rest of us mortals. And the way that they fall! There are serial killers who use their last dying gasps of air trying to save someone else, law breakers who remain smug in false piety and feel comfortable enough passing judgement on those who share the same shitbucket, poser priests who have sold their shepherd staffs and flocks for the wolfish clothing of easy survival and governmental compliance, a conflicted lieutenant who would see his own noble theories and principles violently enforced, and a Judas figure who doesn't even have the good sense enough to hang himself from the nearest tree. And the whiskey priest shares communion with all of them, sharing the bread and the wine in equal amounts.<br /><br /> The story distills itself by the end, culminating in a powerful and heady sip of the inevitable. The whiskey priest manages to "work out his own salvation with fear and with trembling" and abide by the Pauline exclamation that the meaning of death is the same as the meaning of life ("To live is Christ and to die is gain.") It is my understanding that the title also expresses this, through a small bit of the Lord's Prayer: "To thine be the Power and the Glory forever, amen." But, like the whiskey priest, I am open to others' interpretations. <br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11072174412833908748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_10892900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10892900" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/214053-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216438982p2/214053.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10892900?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/214053-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10892900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer423282758034733993">The “whisky priest” is on the run from the law from the law in Mexico. Set in period in Mexico’s history where priests where being shot and the Catholic Church was illegal, this book plays like the New Testament mixed with an existential western. Grim and suspenseful, stocked with cinematic imagery in a gothic and decaying Mexico, this book is masterpiece from the first page on. While my personal beliefs are nearer to the nihilistic lieutenant (kind of a Miltonic devil type character) chasing th</span>
  <span id="freeText423282758034733993" style="display:none">The “whisky priest” is on the run from the law from the law in Mexico. Set in period in Mexico’s history where priests where being shot and the Catholic Church was illegal, this book plays like the New Testament mixed with an existential western. Grim and suspenseful, stocked with cinematic imagery in a gothic and decaying Mexico, this book is masterpiece from the first page on. While my personal beliefs are nearer to the nihilistic lieutenant (kind of a Miltonic devil type character) chasing the priest, I think the ‘whisky priest” is one of literature’s great character. He is flawed and human but dedicated to and personifies his beliefs despite the suicidal risk he is taking. That member of the please-all McSweeney’s generation Rick Moody snarkily dismissed this book as “being too Catholic”. Well, Greene weaves his beliefs more openly into his work than say Flannery O’Conner or T.S. Eliot (early, not that “Four Quartets stuff), people of any or no belief should find this an emotionally and intellectually involving book. This is not a didactic Catholic apology but a troublesome piece of art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="423282758034733993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer12552629732166091626">His best, or my favorite anyway. Listened to the first half driving to St. Louis to care for my mom, the second half on the way back to my daughter’s shower. Read this back in 1998. I liked that the ‘Whiskey Priest’ was traveling as I was. I’m reading it now during my week at home and I may re-listen on my return journey ... there is so much to take in. <br/><br/>Do we ever learn his name? It feels so strange that he has no name.   <span id="freeText12552629732166091626" style="display:none">His best, or my favorite anyway. Listened to the first half driving to St. Louis to care for my mom, the second half on the way back to my daughter’s shower. Read this back in 1998. I liked that the ‘Whiskey Priest’ was traveling as I was. I’m reading it now during my week at home and I may re-listen on my return journey ... there is so much to take in. <br /><br />Do we ever learn his name? It feels so strange that he has no name. And that he cannot repent of the sin which conceived his daughter. I can understand that in a way because we learn so much more from our sins than from our so-called successes. Father Jose is the opposite of Whiskey in that he is settled, not-on-the-run because he ‘took a wife’, capitulated, and now lives as a failed priest. Yet the Whiskey Priest sees him as the better priest, or at least he did. <br /><br />It is a strange, sad story about restlessness and life lived on the run. How the people we meet along the way shape us. I just want to keep reading it.</span>

  <span id="freeText74189999056438837" style="display:none">This novel was a pleasure to read. The unnamed Priest journey of 10 years in escaping death by firing squad reaches it's inevitable end. Greene describes well the moral struggles in the fight between good and evil with both hard to separate. <br /><br />The story is set in Mexico during the 1930s when Catholic priests were persecuted and forced to marry. He struggles mentally and spiritually within himself. <br /><br />The unnamed 'whisky priest' is on a journey of self-destruction. However, in the end he acquires a holiness by giving a confession to a dying man knowing his adversary the unnamed Lieutenant will capture and execute him. <br /><br />Fate plays a major role in the story and chance with the Priest managing several escapes.   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/304094687" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/304094687?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer304094687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5347533623104150155">[9/10] a great book, I could easily have given it 5 stars, but I'm trying to curb my enthusiasm a little, seeing how high my overall rating is. What can I do? I love books and I'm not that difficult to please. Although pleasing is not the first thing that comes to mind about The Power and The Glory.<br/><br/>Disturbing, heart wrenching, gloomy, suicidally downbeat for most of the journey - yet I feel this is a story that needed to be told, one that couldn't be sugar coated with witty remarks or beautiful </span>
  <span id="freeText5347533623104150155" style="display:none">[9/10] a great book, I could easily have given it 5 stars, but I'm trying to curb my enthusiasm a little, seeing how high my overall rating is. What can I do? I love books and I'm not that difficult to please. Although pleasing is not the first thing that comes to mind about The Power and The Glory.<br /><br />Disturbing, heart wrenching, gloomy, suicidally downbeat for most of the journey - yet I feel this is a story that needed to be told, one that couldn't be sugar coated with witty remarks or beautiful phrasing.<br />The first things to meet the reader are the buzzards hanging on top of derelict buildings in a small Mexican town suffocated by relentless heat and poverty. The setting feels like the cemetery of dreams - all dreams, whether they are of monetary success (the dentist) , revolutionary victory (the lieutenant) or redemption (priest) .<br /><br />The Father is a memorable character not in his role as the last priest to escape fatal persecution at the hands of the new government, but in his human frailty. In his own words he is not a martyr and is not trying to become one, but for all his vices and weaknesses, he is still trying to do good for his fellow men, deserving or not. He is also self aware and constantly struggling to reconcile his cowardice with his enduring belief in a higher power. I don't subscribe to any established religion myself (I prefer the term "humanist" to "atheist") , but I have always been interested in the role faith plays both in the individual development and in defining a culture at a certain moment in time. This book provided a lot of food for thought, and while it criticizes some of the most ill advised practices of catholicism (celibacy, greed, intransigence, the doctrine of suffering in this life for rewards in the next one) , it also brings out the best in some people - mercy, tolerance, empathy, selflessness.<br /><br />The book also contains one quote that I have long memorized, without putting it in the context of this particular story:<br /><br /><i> Hate is a failure of the imagination </i><br /><br />If I have any criticism of the book, it will not be about the writing - Greene outdid himself here - but with a little anti-science sermon right by the end. I'm trying not to give spoilers, but it was a claim that miracles still happen today and scientists are refusing to acknowledge them. It felt for me unnecessary to the story so far and poorly argumented.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5347533623104150155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2340487819" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2340487819" />
    <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton"><img alt="Matthew Appleton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578773686p2/57003346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2340487819?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Appleton" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton">Matthew Appleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/30840015-the-power-and-the-glory">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=1001-books-before-you-die">1001-books-before-you-die</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=english-literature">english-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2340487819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4085908210167501207">108th book of 2020.<br/><br/>This is my 9th Greene novel, and after some ‘entertainments’ recently, it was a joy to read Greene on absolute top-form again. For such a small book, at just 220 pages, by my edition, there is a lot to discuss.<br/><br/>Firstly, the plot: “In one of the southern states of Mexico, during an anti-clerical purge, the last priest, like a hunted hare, is on the run.” That is taken from the blurb of my edition. For some reason I disbelieved how much the novel would be, as it’s put, “on the ru</span>
  <span id="freeText4085908210167501207" style="display:none">108th book of 2020.<br /><br />This is my 9th Greene novel, and after some ‘entertainments’ recently, it was a joy to read Greene on absolute top-form again. For such a small book, at just 220 pages, by my edition, there is a lot to discuss.<br /><br />Firstly, the plot: “In one of the southern states of Mexico, during an anti-clerical purge, the last priest, like a hunted hare, is on the run.” That is taken from the blurb of my edition. For some reason I disbelieved how much the novel would be, as it’s put, “on the run.” I had some premonition that the plot would be slow, or dull, or too bogged down by themes of Catholicism (which was a ridiculous thought in itself, for Greene has never ‘bogged’ me down). The novel is very much “on the run”; the priest (for he is given no name) flees the police, settling briefly, getting help where he can. It is vaguely reminiscent of War books that follow Jews in hiding, or soldiers in enemy lines (for example, from earlier this year, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/118139.Love_and_War_in_the_Apennines" title="Love and War in the Apennines by Eric Newby" rel="nofollow noopener">Love and War in the Apennines</a>). The plot is tense. As premises go, though simple, the concept of the chase is just brilliant. As a reader we know there are only two options: escape, without its share of hardships, or capture. But, enough of the abstract.<br /><br />Greene’s writing is never bad, but I often feel that his entertainments are written with plot at its forefront (aptly, to make them entertaining) whereas his other novels, like this one, seem to consider every line. And without just the plot, which I’ve briefly suggested as being tense and engaging, the setting was a very, very pleasant surprise. My closest friend here on Goodreads, and Greene aficionado, mentioned in his own review that this book is like a dystopian. Not only do I agree with that, I agree with it so wholeheartedly that I would like to shed my own light on the matter. But firstly, go and read Zoeb’s review of this novel, and once you’re there, all his reviews of Greene (I believe he’s read all of them now, or close to), for they say everything anyone could possibly hope to say. His review of The Power and the Glory is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2550185680?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. <br /><br /><b>Stop all the clocks</b><br /><br />Dystopian: The novel is set in Mexico, and Greene spent some time there himself in research for this novel (this novel is also long after he was received into the Catholic Church himself). It shows. From the first few pages we are thrown into this wasteland: oppressively hot, dangerous, barren. All the way through the novel I was trying to think of the word that sums it up and Greene handed it to me fairly near the end: desolation. It is a land of desolation. Greene conjures it in many ways. One that stood out for me was the slowness of time, or lack of time altogether. The characters were often left pondering the time, with no way of telling it. Like Auden – <i>Stop all the clocks…</i> I have too many underlined passages in my book to write them all, but I’ll throw in snippets, like broken fragments. I wrote down the side of page 100: “Bleeding feet, no time, fevers, lack of sleep, the chase, grey…” and a final word which I cannot even discern. It looks to me like “hit”, but I can’t for the life of me work out the relevance of that, if it is. Here are some of those promised fragments: <i>they came to the cemetery, full of chipped angels / mosquitos hummed all around like sewing machines / The lieutenant stood there like a little dark menacing question-mark in the sun / Evil ran like malaria in his veins / The beetles had disappeared; the rain had apparently washed them away: it came perpendicularly down, with a sort of measured intensity, as if it were driving nails into a coffin lid.</i> These images: the dark, the thunder, the mosquitos, the rain, the fear of being hunted, all bring about an overwhelming sense of dread that haunts the narrative, as the priest is haunted by thoughts of his capture, and death. <br /><br /><b>What is God like?</b><br /><br />There is no way to discuss this book without discussing the religious debates within it. The priest is known as a ‘whiskey priest’ – he is a bad Catholic: he has a child, he drinks too much, he is arrogant; but does his belief waver in the wake of his chase? <br /><br />The lieutenant is a great antagonist. Greene jumps through several characters throughout the novel. The conversations on God were briefly and clearly constructed; Greene has a gift for being deeply (not falsely) philosophical with the most simple sentences. First, I will share some of the highlights on God and religion, and then the fragments that refer to life itself. More dread is weaved into the narrative by some of the concepts of God: When asked by a child, <i>What is God like?</i> the priest answered with ideas of the father, mother, brother and sister, <i>of all loves and relationships combined in an immense and yet personal passion… But at the centre of his own faith there always stood the convincing mystery – that we were made in God’s image. God was the parent, but He was also the policeman, the criminal, the priest, the maniac, and the judge.</i> Another - <i>It was odd – this fury to deface, because, of course, you could never deface enough. If God had been like a toad, you could have rid the globe of toads, but when God was like yourself, it was no good being content with stone figures – you had to kill yourself among the graves.</i><br /><br /><b>Strange Discoveries a Man can Make</b><br /><br /><i>It is one of the strange discoveries a man can make that life, however you lead it, contains moments of exhilaration; there are always comparisons which can be made with worse times: even in danger and misery the pendulum swings.</i> Quotes like these remind me of Greene’s own battles with mental health, namely, his bipolar. <i>Instinct is like a sense of duty – one can confuse it with loyalty, very easily.</i> Perhaps most brilliantly, Greene writes this: <i>When you visualise a man or woman carefully, you could always begin to feel pity – that was a quality of God’s image carried with it… Hate was just a failure of imagination.</i> <br /><br /><b>Baleful Vulture</b><br /><br />I will not spoil the ending of the book. There are only two outcomes, as I have said, escape or capture. My edition’s blurb ends with this rather dramatic comment I would like to finish on:<br /><br />“A baleful vulture of doom hovers over this modern crucifixion story, but above the vulture soars an eagle – the inevitably of the Church’s triumph.”<br /><br />I will say, despite the hope in this line, it does not answer the question – escape, or death?</span>
  <a data-text-id="4085908210167501207" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2553493071" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2553493071" />
    <a title="Rose Auburn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64708434-rose-auburn"><img alt="Rose Auburn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516184556p2/64708434.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2553493071?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rose Auburn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rose Auburn" href="/user/show/64708434-rose-auburn">Rose Auburn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2553493071" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17778669609151957951">No-one quite writes like Graham Greene, he has the most beautiful, engaging easy style that draws you in from the first word and despite, the ease of reading, gives you some incredibly thought-provoking narrative that probes some pretty deep issues. This book is no exception. It does meander at times but then so does the protagonist and, if you are looking for a cheerful pick-me-up read, this is not it. His description of the hell-hole prison cell is, without doubt, some of the best descriptive </span>
  <span id="freeText17778669609151957951" style="display:none">No-one quite writes like Graham Greene, he has the most beautiful, engaging easy style that draws you in from the first word and despite, the ease of reading, gives you some incredibly thought-provoking narrative that probes some pretty deep issues. This book is no exception. It does meander at times but then so does the protagonist and, if you are looking for a cheerful pick-me-up read, this is not it. His description of the hell-hole prison cell is, without doubt, some of the best descriptive writing I have read, I desperately wanted a shower after reading the passage, I really could taste, smell, see and hear the squalor, it was quite remarkable.<br /><br />It's a book that will linger in and out of my mind for some time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17778669609151957951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

		    <b>
          <a title="Reading 1001" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/19373109-the-power-and-the-glory-by-graham-greene">Reading 1001:</a>
        </b>
      
			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/19373109-the-power-and-the-glory-by-graham-greene">The Power and the Glory, by Graham Greene</a>
		</td>
		<td class="nobreak">
		  1

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/61HecIuXUJZfTKZiTLgTcTPm3k5nUzCbhMNHZg+bbbFiy6reXCUKv5Whvh+x+qSEpB8sfcQwqU1bUusVX2fLA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TMp4+ZZ4Q3k5YuSGPuWDfI54J9cmfa6ax8cY+BmbBRt27BErLSaKEZ5JUOfJEImjWCfAH+jwIP2cuV5J1Nj3gQ==')})
  }

C’è un io e c’è un noi: anzi, un noantri. E c’è un bambino che impara a vivere dalle persone che gli stanno intorno: «gente che non si tiene niente nel cuore», allegra, chiassosa, abituata ad amare anche nei momenti più bui, e ad amare senza misura. Bonvissuto canta la Roma, ma canta soprattutto un amore assoluto, fulminante, che si accende nell’animo per trasformarlo.\n\nMescolando alto e basso a ogni riga, divagazioni e scene formidabili, il nuovo libro di Bonvissuto parte come un trattatello filosofico sull’amore per diventare a poco a poco un romanzo corale di grande forza. A differenza di molte passioni, quella calcistica dura una vita intera e arde sempre, nel bene e soprattutto nel male: «Forse il calcio è l’unica cosa al mondo che è più bella quando la fanno gli altri, quelli con quella maglia però. Che comunque ce l’hanno solo in prestito, perché la maglia della Roma è la mia. Potrebbero anche averla rubata. E l’amore forse è questo: correre appresso a un ladro che ci ha rubato qualcosa». Attorno a questa fiamma si condensa un microcosmo di padri, nonni, zii, fratelli di fede giallorossa, una comunità vera e propria, allegra, sterminata, capace d’iniziarti alla vita. La condivisione delle sconfitte, il divano da cui tutta la famiglia «guarda» la radio, l’epica costruzione della bandiera da portare allo stadio insieme ai panini con la frittata, le trasferte su quel pulmino lentissimo che profuma di mandarini, e le partite, certo, viste con occhi bambini ancora allergici a date, nomi, tecnicismi, ma capaci di vedere pure l’invisibile. Poi c’è Barabba, che vive in una roulotte lungo la ferrovia: spetterà a lui svelare al bambino la quantità di universi concentrati in una sola maglia di calcio. La numero cinque. La indossa un brasiliano atipico, un centrocampista che arriva in punta di piedi e realizza il sogno proibito di tutti i tifosi, l’innominabile parola che inizia con la s… La gioia fa parecchio rumore è uno di quei libri che ti fanno immergere totalmente nel mondo che raccontano. E che te lo fanno rimpiangere, alla fine, come se fosse il tuo.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9018465737684576170\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Collected here are Althusser’s most significant philosophical writings from the late sixties and through the seventies. Intended to contribute, in his own words, to a ‘left-wing critique of Stalinism that would help put some substance back into the revolutionary project here in the West’, they are the record of a shared history. At the same time they chart Althusser’s critique of the theoretical system unveiled in his own major works, and his developing practice of philosophy as a ‘revolutionary weapon’.\n\nThe collection opens with two lucid early articles - &quot;Theory, Theoretical Practice and Theoretical Formation&quot; and &quot;On Theoretical Work.&quot; The title piece - Althusser’s celebrated lectures in the &quot;Philosophy Course for Scientists&quot; — is the fullest exploration of his new definition of philosophy as politics in the realm of theory, a conception which is further developed in &quot;Lenin and Philosophy.&quot; &quot;Is it Simple to be a Marxist in Philosophy?&quot; provides an invaluable account of Althusser’s intellectual development. The volume concludes with two little-known late pieces - &quot;The Transformation of Philosophy,&quot; in which the paradoxical history of Marxist philosopher is investigated; and &quot;Marxism today,&quot; a sober balance-sheet of the Marxist tradition. Attesting to the unique place that Althusser has occupied in modern intellectual history - between a tradition of Marxism that he sought to reconstruct, and a &quot;post-Marxism&quot; that has <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17780251900779514085">Henry Graham Greene, OM, CH was an English novelist, short story writer, playwright, screenplay writer, travel writer and critic whose works explore the ambivalent moral and political issues of the modern world. Greene combined serious literary acclaim with wide popularity.<br/><br/>Although Greene objected strongly to being described as a “Catholic novelist” rather than as a “novelist who happened to be Ca</span>
  <span id="freeText17780251900779514085" style="display:none">Henry Graham Greene, OM, CH was an English novelist, short story writer, playwright, screenplay writer, travel writer and critic whose works explore the ambivalent moral and political issues of the modern world. Greene combined serious literary acclaim with wide popularity.<br /><br />Although Greene objected strongly to being described as a “Catholic novelist” rather than as a “novelist who happened to be Catholic,” Catholic religious themes are at the root of much of his writing, especially the four major Catholic novels: <i>Brighton Rock, The Heart of the Matter, The End of the Affair,</i> and <i>The Power and the Glory.</i> Works such as <i>The Quiet American, Our Man in Havana</i> and <i>The Human Factor</i> also show an avid interest in the workings of international politics and espionage.<br /><br />(<i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graham_Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>)</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17780251900779514085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
